   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    Stress (biology)
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>

      </a>

    </div>

     <p>
      Physiological or biological
      <b>
       stress
      </b>
      is an organism's response to a
      <a href="/wiki/Stressor" title="Stressor">
       stressor
      </a>
      such as an environmental condition. Stress is a body's method of reacting to a
      <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/challenge" title="wikt:challenge">
       challenge
      </a>
      . According to the stressful event, the body's way to respond to stress is by
      <a href="/wiki/Sympathetic_nervous_system" title="Sympathetic nervous system">
       sympathetic nervous system
      </a>
      activation which results in the
      <a href="/wiki/Fight-or-flight_response" title="Fight-or-flight response">
       fight-or-flight response
      </a>
      . Because the body cannot keep this state for long periods of time, the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parasympathetic_system" title="Parasympathetic system">
       parasympathetic system
      </a>
      returns the body's physiological conditions to normal (
      <a href="/wiki/Homeostasis" title="Homeostasis">
       homeostasis
      </a>
      ). In
      <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">
       humans
      </a>
      , stress typically describes a negative condition that can affect a person's
      <a href="/wiki/Mind" title="Mind">
       mental
      </a>
      and physical
      <a href="/wiki/Quality_of_life" title="Quality of life">
       well-being
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

        </a>

     <p>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">
       Overview
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Overview">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-move" role="presentation">
      <tr>
       <td class="mbox-image">
        <div style="width:52px">
         <img alt="" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="60" height="20" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Split-arrows.svg/60px-Split-arrows.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Split-arrows.svg/90px-Split-arrows.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Split-arrows.svg/120px-Split-arrows.svg.png 2x" width="60"/>
        </div>
       </td>
       <td class="mbox-text">
        <span class="mbox-text-span">
         <b>
          This article or section may need to be cleaned up.
         </b>
         It has been merged from
         <i>
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stress_(psychology)" title="Stress (psychology)">
           Stress (psychology)#Impact on health
          </a>
         </i>
         .
        </span>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      There is likely a connection between stress and illness. Theories of the stress–illness link suggest that both acute and chronic stress can cause illness, and several studies found such a link.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schneiderman.2C_N._2005_1-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Schneiderman.2C_N._2005-1">
        [1]
       </a>
      </sup>
      According to these theories, both kinds of stress can lead to changes in behavior and in physiology. Behavioral changes can be smoking and eating habits and physical activity. Physiological changes can be changes in
      <a href="/wiki/Sympathetic_nervous_system" title="Sympathetic nervous system">
       sympathetic
      </a>
      activation or
      <a href="/wiki/Hypothalamic%E2%80%93pituitary%E2%80%93adrenal_axis" title="Hypothalamic–pituitary–adrenal axis">
       hypothalamic pituitary adrenocorticoid
      </a>
      activation, and immunological function.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-2">
        [2]
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, there is much variability in the link between stress and illness.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ogden_3-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ogden-3">
        [3]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Selye demonstrated that stress decreases adaptability of an organism and proposed to describe the adaptability as a special resource,
      <i>
       adaptation energy
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SelyeAE1_4-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-SelyeAE1-4">
        [4]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In recent works, it is considered as an internal coordinate on the "dominant path" in the model of adaptation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GorbanJTB2016_5-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-GorbanJTB2016-5">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Stress can make the individual more susceptible to physical illnesses like the common cold.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-6">
        [6]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Stressful events, such as job changes, may result in insomnia, impaired sleeping, and health complaints.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-7">
        [7]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Research indicates the type of stressor (whether it's acute or chronic) and individual characteristics such as age and physical well-being before the onset of the stressor can combine to determine the effect of stress on an individual.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schneiderman.2C_N._2005_1-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Schneiderman.2C_N._2005-1">
        [1]
       </a>
      </sup>
      An individual's personality characteristics (such as level of neuroticism),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Neuroticism2014_8-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Neuroticism2014-8">
        [8]
       </a>
      </sup>
      genetics, and childhood experiences with major stressors and traumas
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jeronimus2013_9-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Jeronimus2013-9">
        [9]
       </a>
      </sup>
      may also dictate their response to stressors.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schneiderman.2C_N._2005_1-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Schneiderman.2C_N._2005-1">
        [1]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Chronic stress and a lack of coping resources available or used by an individual can often lead to the development of psychological issues such as
      <a href="/wiki/Major_depressive_disorder" title="Major depressive disorder">
       depression
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Anxiety_disorder" title="Anxiety disorder">
       anxiety
      </a>
      (see below for further information).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-10">
        [10]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This is particularly true regarding chronic stressors. These are stressors that may not be as intense as an acute stressor like a natural disaster or a major accident, but they persist over longer periods of time. These types of stressors tend to have a more negative effect on health because they are sustained and thus require the body's physiological response to occur daily. This depletes the body's energy more quickly and usually occurs over long periods of time, especially when these microstressors cannot be avoided (i.e.- stress of living in a dangerous neighborhood). See
      <a href="/wiki/Allostatic_load" title="Allostatic load">
       allostatic load
      </a>
      for further discussion of the biological process by which chronic stress may affect the body. For example, studies have found that caregivers, particularly those of dementia patients, have higher levels of depression and slightly worse physical health than noncaregivers.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-11">
        [11]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Studies have also showed that psychological stress may directly contribute to the disproportionately high rates of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coronary_heart_disease" title="Coronary heart disease">
       coronary heart disease
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Morbidity" title="Morbidity">
       morbidity
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Mortality_rate" title="Mortality rate">
       mortality
      </a>
      and its etiologic
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Risk_factors" title="Risk factors">
       risk factors
      </a>
      . Specifically, acute and chronic stress have been shown to raise
      <a href="/wiki/Hypercholesterolemia" title="Hypercholesterolemia">
       serum lipids
      </a>
      and are associated with clinical coronary events.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calderon_12-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Calderon-12">
        [12]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      However, it is possible for individuals to exhibit
      <a href="/wiki/Hardiness_(psychological)" title="Hardiness (psychological)">
       hardiness
      </a>
      —a term referring to the ability to be both chronically stressed and healthy.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
       <a href="#cite_note-13">
        [13]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Many psychologists are currently interested in studying the factors that allow hardy individuals to cope with stress and evade most health and illness problems associated with high levels of stress. Stress can be associated with psychological disorders such as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/General_anxiety_disorder" title="General anxiety disorder">
       general anxiety disorder
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Depression_(mood)" title="Depression (mood)">
       depression
      </a>
      , and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Post-traumatic_stress_disorder" title="Post-traumatic stress disorder">
       post-traumatic stress disorder
      </a>
      . However, it is important to note that everyone experiences some level of stress, and diagnosis of stress disorders can only be performed by a licensed practitioner.
     </p>
     <p>
      It has long been believed that negative affective states, such as feelings of anxiety and depression, could influence the pathogenesis of physical disease, which in turn, have direct effects on biological process that could result in increased risk of disease in the end. However recent studies done by the University of Wisconsin-Madison and other places have shown this to be untrue, it isn't stress itself that causes the increased risk of illness or death, it is actually the
      <i>
       perception
      </i>
      that stress is harmful.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-14">
        [14]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blog.ted.com_15-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-blog.ted.com-15">
        [15]
       </a>
      </sup>
      For example, when humans are under chronic stress, permanent changes in their physiological, emotional, and behavioral responses are most likely to occur.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jeronimus2014_16-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Jeronimus2014-16">
        [16]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohen2007_17-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Cohen2007-17">
        [17]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Such changes could lead to disease. Chronic stress results from stressful events that persist over a relatively long period of time, such as caring for a spouse with dementia, or results from brief focal events that continue to be experienced as overwhelmingly long after they are over, such as experiencing a sexual assault.
     </p>
     <p>
      Experiments
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      show that when healthy human individuals are exposed to acute laboratory stressors, they show an adaptive enhancement of some markers of natural immunity but a general suppression of functions of specific immunity. By comparison, when healthy human individuals are exposed to real-life chronic stress, this stress is associated with a biphasic immune response where partial suppression of cellular and humoral function coincides with low-grade, nonspecific inflammation.
     </p>
     <p>
      Even though psychological stress is often connected with illness or disease, most healthy individuals can still remain disease-free after confronting chronic stressful events. Also, people who do not believe that stress will affect their health do not have an increased risk of illness, disease, or death.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blog.ted.com_15-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-blog.ted.com-15">
        [15]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This suggests that there are individual differences in vulnerability to the potential pathogenic effects of stress; individual differences in vulnerability arise due to both genetic and psychological factors. In addition, the age at which the stress is experienced can dictate its effect on health. Research suggests chronic stress at a young age can have lifelong effects on the biological, psychological, and behavioral responses to stress later in life.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
       <a href="#cite_note-18">
        [18]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Recent studies have shown that severe psychological stress resulting in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PTSD" title="PTSD">
       PTSD
      </a>
      can also significantly affect parenting perception, behavior, neural activity and HPA-axis physiology in response to stressful parent-infant interactions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
       <a href="#cite_note-19">
        [19]
       </a>
      </sup>
      These recent studies support the existence of intergenerational effects of early chronic psychological stress.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
       <a href="#cite_note-20">
        [20]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In animals, stress contributes to the initiation, growth, and metastasis of select tumors, but studies that try to link stress and cancer incidence in humans have had mixed results. This can be due to practical difficulties in designing and implementing adequate studies.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohen2007_17-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Cohen2007-17">
        [17]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology_and_historical_usage">
       Etymology and historical usage
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Etymology and historical usage">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      The term "stress" had none of its contemporary connotations before the 1920s. It is a form of the
      <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">
       Middle English
      </a>
      <i>
       destresse
      </i>
      , derived via
      <a href="/wiki/Old_French" title="Old French">
       Old French
      </a>
      from the
      <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">
       Latin
      </a>
      <i>
       stringere
      </i>
      , "to draw tight."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keil2004_21-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Keil2004-21">
        [21]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The word had long been in use in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stress_(physics)" title="Stress (physics)">
       physics
      </a>
      to refer to the internal distribution of a force exerted on a material body, resulting in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Strain_(materials_science)" title="Strain (materials science)">
       strain
      </a>
      . In the 1920s and '30s, biological and psychological circles occasionally used the term to refer to a mental strain or to a harmful environmental agent that could cause illness.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Walter_Cannon" title="Walter Cannon">
       Walter Cannon
      </a>
      used it in 1926 to refer to external factors that disrupted what he called
      <a href="/wiki/Homeostasis" title="Homeostasis">
       homeostasis
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
       <a href="#cite_note-22">
        [22]
       </a>
      </sup>
      But "...stress as an explanation of lived experience is absent from both lay and expert life narratives before the 1930s".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
       <a href="#cite_note-23">
        [23]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Physiological stress represents a wide range of physical responses that occur as a direct effect of a stressor causing an upset in the homeostasis of the body. Upon immediate disruption of either psychological or physical equilibrium the body responds by stimulating the
      <a href="/wiki/Nervous_system" title="Nervous system">
       nervous
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Endocrine_system" title="Endocrine system">
       endocrine
      </a>
      , and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immune_systems" title="Immune systems">
       immune systems
      </a>
      . The reaction of these systems causes a number of physical changes that have both short- and long-term effects on the body.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2015)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Biological_need_for_equilibrium">
       Biological need for equilibrium
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Biological need for equilibrium">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Homeostasis" title="Homeostasis">
       Homeostasis
      </a>
      is a concept central to the idea of stress.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
       <a href="#cite_note-24">
        [24]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In
      <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">
       biology
      </a>
      , most
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biochemical" title="Biochemical">
       biochemical
      </a>
      processes strive to maintain equilibrium (homeostasis), a steady state that exists more as an ideal and less as an achievable condition. Environmental factors, internal or external stimuli, continually disrupt homeostasis; an organism's present condition is a state of constant flux moving about a homeostatic point that is that organism's optimal condition for living.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
       <a href="#cite_note-25">
        [25]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Factors causing an organism's condition to diverge too far from homeostasis can be experienced as stress. A life-threatening situation such as a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Major_physical_trauma" title="Major physical trauma">
       major physical trauma
      </a>
      or prolonged
      <a href="/wiki/Starvation" title="Starvation">
       starvation
      </a>
      can greatly disrupt homeostasis. On the other hand, an organism's attempt at restoring conditions back to or near homeostasis, often consuming
      <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">
       energy
      </a>
      and natural resources, can also be interpreted as stress.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
       <a href="#cite_note-26">
        [26]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The ambiguity in defining this phenomenon was first recognized by
      <a href="/wiki/Hans_Selye" title="Hans Selye">
       Hans Selye
      </a>
      (1907-1982) in 1926. In 1951 a commentator loosely summarized Selye's view of stress as something that "…in addition to being itself, was also the cause of itself, and the result of itself."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
       <a href="#cite_note-27">
        [27]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
       <a href="#cite_note-28">
        [28]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      First to use the term in a biological context, Selye continued to define stress as "the non-specific response of the body to any demand placed upon it". As of 2011
      <sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">
       <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit">
        [update]
       </a>
      </sup>
      neuroscientists such as
      <a href="/wiki/Bruce_McEwen" title="Bruce McEwen">
       Bruce McEwen
      </a>
      and Jaap Koolhaas believe that stress, based on years of empirical research, "should be restricted to conditions where an environmental demand exceeds the natural regulatory capacity of an organism".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
       <a href="#cite_note-29">
        [29]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Biological_background">
       Biological background
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Biological background">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Stress can have many profound effects on the human biological systems.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
       <a href="#cite_note-30">
        [30]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Biology primarily attempts to explain major concepts of stress using a stimulus-response paradigm, broadly comparable to how a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Psychobiological" title="Psychobiological">
       psychobiological
      </a>
      sensory system operates. The
      <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">
       central nervous system
      </a>
      (brain and spinal cord) plays a crucial role in the body's stress-related mechanisms. Whether one should interpret these mechanisms as the body's response to a
      <a href="/wiki/Stressor" title="Stressor">
       stressor
      </a>
      or embody the act of stress itself is part of the ambiguity in defining what exactly stress is. Nevertheless, the central nervous system works closely with the body's
      <a href="/wiki/Endocrine_system" title="Endocrine system">
       endocrine system
      </a>
      to regulate these mechanisms. The
      <a href="/wiki/Sympathetic_nervous_system" title="Sympathetic nervous system">
       sympathetic nervous system
      </a>
      becomes primarily active during a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stress_response" title="Stress response">
       stress response
      </a>
      , regulating many of the body's
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physiological" title="Physiological">
       physiological
      </a>
      functions in ways that ought to make an organism more
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adaptive" title="Adaptive">
       adaptive
      </a>
      to its environment. Below there follows a brief biological background of
      <a href="/wiki/Neuroanatomy" title="Neuroanatomy">
       neuroanatomy
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Neurochemistry" title="Neurochemistry">
       neurochemistry
      </a>
      and how they relate to stress.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2015)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Stress, either severe, acute stress or chronic low-grade stress may induce abnormalities in three principal regulatory systems in the body:
      <a href="/wiki/Serotonin" title="Serotonin">
       serotonin systems
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Catecholamine" title="Catecholamine">
       catecholamine systems
      </a>
      , and the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypothalamic-pituitary-adrenocortical_axis" title="Hypothalamic-pituitary-adrenocortical axis">
       hypothalamic-pituitary-adrenocortical axis
      </a>
      .
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aggressive_behavior" title="Aggressive behavior">
       Aggressive behavior
      </a>
      has also been associated with abnormalities in these systems.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
       <a href="#cite_note-31">
        [31]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Neuroanatomy">
       Neuroanatomy
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Neuroanatomy">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rotating_brain_colored.gif">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="320" data-file-width="320" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Rotating_brain_colored.gif/220px-Rotating_brain_colored.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9c/Rotating_brain_colored.gif 1.5x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rotating_brain_colored.gif" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Human brain: Hypothalamus =
        <span style="background-color:Red; color:; border:1px solid darkgray; text-align:center;">
        </span>
        , amygdala =
        <span style="background-color:LawnGreen; color:; border:1px solid darkgray; text-align:center;">
        </span>
        , hippocampus/
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fornix_of_the_brain" title="Fornix of the brain">
         fornix
        </a>
        =
        <span style="background-color:RoyalBlue; color:; border:1px solid darkgray; text-align:center;">
        </span>
        , pons=
        <span style="background-color:Gold; color:; border:1px solid darkgray; text-align:center;">
        </span>
        , pituitary gland=
        <span style="background-color:Magenta; color:; border:1px solid darkgray; text-align:center;">
        </span>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Brain">
       Brain
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Brain">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Brain" title="Brain">
       brain
      </a>
      plays a critical role in the body's perception of and response to stress.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
       <a href="#cite_note-32">
        [32]
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, pinpointing exactly which regions of the brain are responsible for particular aspects of a stress response is difficult and often unclear. Understanding that the brain works in more of a network-like fashion carrying information about a stressful situation across regions of the brain (from cortical sensory areas to more basal structures and vice versa) can help explain how stress and its negative consequences are heavily rooted in neural communication dysfunction. In spite of this, several important brain structures implicated in playing key roles in stress response pathways are described below:
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Hypothalamus">
       Hypothalamus
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Hypothalamus">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Hypothalamus" title="Hypothalamus">
       hypothalamus
      </a>
      is a small portion of the brain located below the
      <a href="/wiki/Thalamus" title="Thalamus">
       thalamus
      </a>
      and above the
      <a href="/wiki/Brainstem" title="Brainstem">
       brainstem
      </a>
      . One of its most important functions is to help link together the body's nervous and endocrine systems. This structure has many bidirectional neural inputs and outputs from and to various other brain regions. These connections help to regulate the hypothalamus' ability to secrete
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hormones" title="Hormones">
       hormones
      </a>
      into the body's
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blood_stream" title="Blood stream">
       blood stream
      </a>
      , having far-reaching and long-lasting effects on physiological processes such as
      <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">
       metabolism
      </a>
      . During a stress response, the hypothalamus secretes various hormones, namely
      <a href="/wiki/Corticotropin-releasing_hormone" title="Corticotropin-releasing hormone">
       corticotropin-releasing hormone
      </a>
      , which stimulates the body's pituitary gland and initiates a heavily regulated stress response pathway.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
       <a href="#cite_note-33">
        [33]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Amygdala">
       Amygdala
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Amygdala">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Amygdala" title="Amygdala">
       amygdala
      </a>
      is a small, "almond"-shaped structure, two of which are located bilaterally and deep within the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medial_temporal_lobe" title="Medial temporal lobe">
       medial temporal lobes
      </a>
      of the brain. The amygdalae are part of the brain's
      <a href="/wiki/Limbic_system" title="Limbic system">
       limbic system
      </a>
      , with projections to and from the hypothalamus, hippocampus, and locus coeruleus among other areas. Thought to play a role in the processing of emotions, the amygdalae have been implicated in modulating stress response mechanisms, particularly when feelings of anxiety or fear are involved.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
       <a href="#cite_note-34">
        [34]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Hippocampus">
       Hippocampus
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Hippocampus">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Hippocampus" title="Hippocampus">
       hippocampus
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
       <a href="#cite_note-35">
        [35]
       </a>
      </sup>
      is a structure located bilaterally, deep within the medial temporal lobes of the brain, just below each amygdala,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
       <a href="#cite_note-36">
        [36]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and is a part of the brain's limbic system. The hippocampus is thought to play an important role in
      <a href="/wiki/Memory" title="Memory">
       memory
      </a>
      formation. There are numerous connections to the hippocampus from the
      <a href="/wiki/Cerebral_cortex" title="Cerebral cortex">
       cerebral cortex
      </a>
      , hypothalamus, and amygdala, among other regions. During stress, the hippocampus is particularly important, in that
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cognitive" title="Cognitive">
       cognitive
      </a>
      processes such as prior memories can have a great influence on enhancing, suppressing, or even independently generating a stress response. The hippocampus is also an area in the brain that is susceptible to damage brought upon by chronic stress.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
       <a href="#cite_note-37">
        [37]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Prefrontal_cortex">
       Prefrontal cortex
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Prefrontal cortex">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Prefrontal_cortex" title="Prefrontal cortex">
       prefrontal cortex
      </a>
      , located in the
      <a href="/wiki/Frontal_lobe" title="Frontal lobe">
       frontal lobe
      </a>
      , is the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anterior" title="Anterior">
       anterior
      </a>
      -most region of the
      <a href="/wiki/Cerebral_cortex" title="Cerebral cortex">
       cerebral cortex
      </a>
      . An important function of the prefrontal cortex is to regulate cognitive processes including planning,
      <a href="/wiki/Attention" title="Attention">
       attention
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Problem_solving" title="Problem solving">
       problem solving
      </a>
      through extensive connections with other brain regions. The prefrontal cortex can become impaired during the stress response.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
       <a href="#cite_note-38">
        [38]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
       <a href="#cite_note-39">
        [39]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This has been shown to be the case among post-traumatically stressed mothers in response to video-stimuli depicting mother-child separation versus play and related to a maternal epigenetic signature of the glucocorticoid receptor gene.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
       <a href="#cite_note-40">
        [40]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Locus_coeruleus">
       Locus coeruleus
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Locus coeruleus">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Locus_coeruleus" title="Locus coeruleus">
       locus coeruleus
      </a>
      is an area located in the
      <a href="/wiki/Pons" title="Pons">
       pons
      </a>
      of the brainstem that is the principal site of the
      <a href="/wiki/Biosynthesis" title="Biosynthesis">
       synthesis
      </a>
      of the
      <a href="/wiki/Neurotransmitter" title="Neurotransmitter">
       neurotransmitter
      </a>
      norepinephrine, which plays an important role in the sympathetic nervous system's fight-or-flight response to stress. This area receives input from the hypothalamus, amygdala, and raphe nucleus among other regions and projects widely across the brain as well as to the spinal cord.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Raphe_nucleus">
       Raphe nucleus
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Raphe nucleus">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Raphe_nucleus" title="Raphe nucleus">
       raphe nucleus
      </a>
      is an area located in the pons of the brainstem that is the principal site of the
      <a href="/wiki/Biosynthesis" title="Biosynthesis">
       synthesis
      </a>
      of the
      <a href="/wiki/Neurotransmitter" title="Neurotransmitter">
       neurotransmitter
      </a>
      serotonin, which plays an important role in
      <a href="/wiki/Mood_(psychology)" title="Mood (psychology)">
       mood
      </a>
      regulation, particularly when stress is associated with
      <a href="/wiki/Depression_(mood)" title="Depression (mood)">
       depression
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Anxiety" title="Anxiety">
       anxiety
      </a>
      . Projections extend from this region to widespread areas across the brain, namely the hypothalamus, and are thought to modulate an organism's
      <a href="/wiki/Circadian_rhythm" title="Circadian rhythm">
       circadian rhythm
      </a>
      and sensation of
      <a href="/wiki/Pain" title="Pain">
       pain
      </a>
      among other processes.
     </p>
     <p>
      <br/>
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dt>
       <b>
        The Spinal Cord
       </b>
      </dt>
     </dl>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:102px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gray_111_-_Vertebral_column-coloured.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="233" height="343" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Gray_111_-_Vertebral_column-coloured.png/100px-Gray_111_-_Vertebral_column-coloured.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Gray_111_-_Vertebral_column-coloured.png/150px-Gray_111_-_Vertebral_column-coloured.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Gray_111_-_Vertebral_column-coloured.png/200px-Gray_111_-_Vertebral_column-coloured.png 2x" width="100"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gray_111_-_Vertebral_column-coloured.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Human spinal cord
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Spinal_cord">
       Spinal cord
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Spinal cord">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Spinal_cord" title="Spinal cord">
       spinal cord
      </a>
      plays a critical role in transferring stress response neural impulses from the brain to the rest of the body. In addition to the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neuroendocrine" title="Neuroendocrine">
       neuroendocrine
      </a>
      blood hormone signaling system initiated by the hypothalamus, the spinal cord communicates with the rest of the body by innervating the
      <a href="/wiki/Peripheral_nervous_system" title="Peripheral nervous system">
       peripheral nervous system
      </a>
      . Certain nerves that belong to the sympathetic branch of the central nervous system exit the spinal cord and stimulate peripheral nerves, which in turn engage the body's major
      <a href="/wiki/Organ_(anatomy)" title="Organ (anatomy)">
       organs
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muscles" title="Muscles">
       muscles
      </a>
      in a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fight-or-flight" title="Fight-or-flight">
       fight-or-flight
      </a>
      manner.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Pituitary_gland">
       Pituitary gland
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Pituitary gland">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Pituitary_gland" title="Pituitary gland">
       pituitary gland
      </a>
      is a small organ that is located at the base of the brain just under the hypothalamus. This gland releases various hormones that play significant roles in regulating
      <a href="/wiki/Homeostasis" title="Homeostasis">
       homeostasis
      </a>
      . During a stress response, the pituitary gland releases hormones into the blood stream, namely
      <a href="/wiki/Adrenocorticotropic_hormone" title="Adrenocorticotropic hormone">
       adrenocorticotropic hormone
      </a>
      , which modulates a heavily regulated stress response system.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Adrenal_gland">
       Adrenal gland
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Adrenal gland">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Illu_adrenal_gland.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="240" data-file-width="206" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Illu_adrenal_gland.jpg/150px-Illu_adrenal_gland.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/14/Illu_adrenal_gland.jpg 1.5x" width="150"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Illu_adrenal_gland.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Adrenal gland
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Adrenal_gland" title="Adrenal gland">
       adrenal gland
      </a>
      is a major organ of the endocrine system that is located directly on top of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kidneys" title="Kidneys">
       kidneys
      </a>
      and is chiefly responsible for the synthesis of stress hormones that are released into the blood stream during a stress response.
      <a href="/wiki/Cortisol" title="Cortisol">
       Cortisol
      </a>
      is the major stress hormone released by the adrenal gland.
     </p>
     <p>
      In addition to the locus coeruleus existing as a source of the neurotransmitter norepinephrine within the central nervous system, the adrenal gland can also release norepinephrine during a stress response into the body's blood stream, at which point norepinephrine acts as a hormone in the endocrine system.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Neurochemistry">
       Neurochemistry
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Neurochemistry">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Corticotropin-releasing_hormone">
       Corticotropin-releasing hormone
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Corticotropin-releasing hormone">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Corticotropin-releasing_hormone" title="Corticotropin-releasing hormone">
       Corticotropin-releasing hormone
      </a>
      is the neurohormone secreted by the hypothalamus during a stress response that stimulates the anterior lobe of the pituitary gland by binding to its corticotropin-releasing hormone-
      <a href="/wiki/Receptor_(biochemistry)" title="Receptor (biochemistry)">
       receptors
      </a>
      , causing the anterior pituitary to release adrenocorticotropic hormone.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
       <a href="#cite_note-41">
        [41]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Adrenocorticotropic_hormone">
       Adrenocorticotropic hormone
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Adrenocorticotropic hormone">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Adrenocorticotropic_hormone" title="Adrenocorticotropic hormone">
       Adrenocorticotropic hormone
      </a>
      is the hormone secreted by the anterior lobe of the pituitary gland into the body's blood stream that stimulates the cortex of the adrenal gland by binding to its adrenocorticotropic hormone-receptors, thus causing the adrenal gland to release cortisol.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Cortisol">
       Cortisol
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Cortisol">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cortisol2.svg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="293" data-file-width="440" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Cortisol2.svg/220px-Cortisol2.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Cortisol2.svg/330px-Cortisol2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Cortisol2.svg/440px-Cortisol2.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cortisol2.svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The atomic structure of the
        <a href="/wiki/Steroid" title="Steroid">
         steroid
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Cortisol" title="Cortisol">
         Cortisol
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Cortisol" title="Cortisol">
       Cortisol
      </a>
      is a
      <a href="/wiki/Steroid" title="Steroid">
       steroid
      </a>
      hormone, belonging to a broader class of steroids called
      <a href="/wiki/Glucocorticoid" title="Glucocorticoid">
       glucocorticoids
      </a>
      , produced by the adrenal gland and secreted during a stress response. Its primary function is to redistribute energy (
      <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">
       glucose
      </a>
      ) to regions of the body that need it most (i.e., the brain and major muscles during a fight-or-flight situation). As a part of the body's fight-or-flight response, cortisol also acts to suppress the body's
      <a href="/wiki/Immune_system" title="Immune system">
       immune system
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Cortisol is synthesized from cholesterol in the adrenal cortex.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
       <a href="#cite_note-42">
        [42]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Its primary function is to increase blood sugar through gluconeogenesis, suppress the immune system and aid in fat and protein metabolism.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
       <a href="#cite_note-43">
        [43]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Norepinephrine">
       Norepinephrine
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Norepinephrine">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Norepinephrine" title="Norepinephrine">
       Norepinephrine
      </a>
      is a neurotransmitter released from locus coeruleus when stimulated by the hypothalamus during a stress response. Norepinephrine serves as the primary chemical messenger of the central nervous system's sympathetic branch that prepares the body for fight-or-flight response.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Serotonin">
       Serotonin
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Serotonin">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Serotonin" title="Serotonin">
       Serotonin
      </a>
      is a neurotransmitter synthesized in the raphe nucleus of the pons of the brainstem and projects to most brain areas. Serotonin is thought to play an important role in mood regulation. Stress-induced serotonin dysfunctions have been associated with anxiety, fear and depression-like symptoms.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Neuropeptide_Y">
       Neuropeptide Y
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Neuropeptide Y">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Neuropeptide_Y" title="Neuropeptide Y">
       Neuropeptide Y
      </a>
      is a
      <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">
       protein
      </a>
      that is synthesized in the hypothalamus and acts as a chemical messenger in the brain. Traditionally, it has been thought to play an important role in
      <a href="/wiki/Appetite" title="Appetite">
       appetite
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feeding_behavior" title="Feeding behavior">
       feeding behavior
      </a>
      , and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Satiety" title="Satiety">
       satiety
      </a>
      , but more recent findings have implicated Neuropeptide Y in anxiety and stress, specifically, stress resiliency.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
       <a href="#cite_note-44">
        [44]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Biological_mechanisms">
       Biological mechanisms
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Biological mechanisms">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
      <tr>
       <td class="mbox-image">
        <div style="width:52px">

        </div>

     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Nervous_system">
       Nervous system
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Nervous system">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <dl>
      <dt>
       Peripheral nervous system (PNS)
      </dt>
     </dl>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Peripheral_nervous_system" title="Peripheral nervous system">
       peripheral nervous system
      </a>
      (PNS) consists of two subsystems: the
      <a href="/wiki/Somatic_nervous_system" title="Somatic nervous system">
       somatic nervous system
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Autonomic_nervous_system" title="Autonomic nervous system">
       autonomic nervous system
      </a>
      . When a physical stressor acts upon the body the sensory-somatic nervous system is triggered through stimulation of the body's
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sensory_nerves" title="Sensory nerves">
       sensory nerves
      </a>
      . The signal acts as a nerve impulse and travels through the body in a process of electrical cell-to-cell communication until it reaches the
      <a href="/wiki/Autonomic_nervous_system" title="Autonomic nervous system">
       autonomic nervous system
      </a>
      . Activation of the autonomic nervous system immediately triggers a series of involuntary chemical responses throughout the body.
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Preganglionic_neurons" title="Preganglionic neurons">
       Preganglionic neurons
      </a>
      release the neurotransmitter
      <a href="/wiki/Acetylcholine" title="Acetylcholine">
       acetylcholine
      </a>
      (ACh). This stimulates
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Postganglionic_neurons" title="Postganglionic neurons">
       postganglionic neurons
      </a>
      which release
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Noradrenaline" title="Noradrenaline">
       noradrenaline
      </a>
      . The noradrenaline flows directly into the bloodstream ensuring that all cells in the body's nervous and endocrine systems have been activated even in areas which the ganglionic neurons are unable to reach.
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dt>
       Endocrine system
      </dt>
     </dl>
     <p>
      When a stressor acts upon the body, the
      <a href="/wiki/Endocrine_system" title="Endocrine system">
       endocrine system
      </a>
      is triggered by the release of the
      <a href="/wiki/Neurotransmitter" title="Neurotransmitter">
       neurotransmitter
      </a>
      noradrenaline by the
      <a href="/wiki/Autonomic_nervous_system" title="Autonomic nervous system">
       autonomic nervous system
      </a>
      . Noradrenaline stimulates the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypothalamic-pituitary-adrenal_axis" title="Hypothalamic-pituitary-adrenal axis">
       hypothalamic-pituitary-adrenal axis
      </a>
      (HPA) which processes the information about the stressor in the
      <a href="/wiki/Hypothalamus" title="Hypothalamus">
       hypothalamus
      </a>
      . This quickly signals the
      <a href="/wiki/Pituitary_gland" title="Pituitary gland">
       pituitary gland
      </a>
      and finally triggers the
      <a href="/wiki/Adrenal_cortex" title="Adrenal cortex">
       adrenal cortex
      </a>
      . The adrenal cortex responds by signaling the release of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corticosteroids" title="Corticosteroids">
       corticosteroids
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Cortisol" title="Cortisol">
       cortisol
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corticotropin_releasing_hormone" title="Corticotropin releasing hormone">
       corticotropin releasing hormone
      </a>
      (CRH) directly into the bloodstream.
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dt>
       Central nervous system (CNS)
      </dt>
     </dl>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">
       central nervous system
      </a>
      (CNS) is made up of the brain and the spinal cord. The brain is equipped to process stress in three main areas: the
      <a href="/wiki/Amygdala" title="Amygdala">
       amygdala
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Hippocampus" title="Hippocampus">
       hippocampus
      </a>
      , and the
      <a href="/wiki/Prefrontal_cortex" title="Prefrontal cortex">
       prefrontal cortex
      </a>
      . Each of these areas is densely packed with stress
      <a href="/wiki/Corticosteroid" title="Corticosteroid">
       corticosteroid
      </a>
      receptors which process the intensity of physical and psychological stressors acting upon the body through a process of hormone reception. There are two types of corticosteroid receptors: mineralocorticoid receptors and glucocorticoid receptors. The
      <a href="/wiki/Mineralocorticoid_receptor" title="Mineralocorticoid receptor">
       mineralocorticoid receptors
      </a>
      (MR) have an extremely high affinity for
      <a href="/wiki/Cortisol" title="Cortisol">
       cortisol
      </a>
      . This means that they are at least partially stimulated at all times and therefore are entirely activated almost immediately when a true stressor is disrupting the homeostasis of the body. The second type of receptor,
      <a href="/wiki/Glucocorticoid_receptor" title="Glucocorticoid receptor">
       glucocorticoid receptors
      </a>
      (GR), have a low affinity for cortisol and only begin to become activated as the sensation of stress reaches its peak intensity on the brain.
     </p>
     <p>
      Stress dramatically reduces the ability of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blood_brain_barrier" title="Blood brain barrier">
       blood brain barrier
      </a>
      (BBB) to block the transfer of chemicals including hormones from entering the brain from the bloodstream. Therefore when corticosteroids are released into the bloodstream – they are immediately able to penetrate the brain and bind to first the MR and then the GR. As the GR begin to become activated, neurons in the amygdala, hippocampus, and prefrontal cortex become over stimulated. This stimulation of the neurons triggers a
      <a href="/wiki/Fight-or-flight_response" title="Fight-or-flight response">
       fight-or-flight response
      </a>
      which allows the brain to quickly process information and therefore deal with life-threatening situations. If the stress response continues and becomes chronic, the hyperactivity of the neurons begins to physically change the brain and have severe damaging effects on one's mental health. As the neurons begin to become stimulated, calcium is released through channels in their cell membranes. Although initially this allows the cells chemical signals to continue to fire, allowing nerve cells to remain stimulated, if this continues the cells will become overloaded with calcium leading to over-firing of neuron signals. The over-firing of the neurons is seen to the brain as a dangerous malfunction; therefore, triggering the cells to shut down to avoid death due to over stimulation.
     </p>
     <p>
      Decline in both
      <a href="/wiki/Neuroplasticity" title="Neuroplasticity">
       neuroplasticity
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Long-term_potentiation" title="Long-term potentiation">
       long-term potentiation
      </a>
      (LTP) occurs in humans after experiencing levels of high continual stress. To maintain homeostasis the brain is continuously forming new neural connections, reorganizing its neural pathways, and working to fix damages caused by injury and disease. This keeps the brain vital and able to perform cognitive complex thinking. When the brain receives a distress signal it immediately begins to go into overdrive. Neural pathways begin to fire and rewire at hyper-speed to help the brain understand how to handle the task at hand. Often, the brain becomes so intently focused on this one task that it is unable to comprehend, learn, or cognitively understand any other sensory information that is being thrown at it during this time. This over stimulation in specific areas and extreme lack of use in others causes several physiological changes in the brain to take place which overall reduce or even destroy the neuroplasticity of the brain.
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dendritic_spines" title="Dendritic spines">
       Dendritic spines
      </a>
      found of the
      <a href="/wiki/Dendrite" title="Dendrite">
       dendrite
      </a>
      of neurons begin to disappear and many dendrites become shorter and even less complex in structure.
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glia_cells" title="Glia cells">
       Glia cells
      </a>
      begin to atrophy and
      <a href="/wiki/Neurogenesis" title="Neurogenesis">
       neurogenesis
      </a>
      often ceases completely. Without neuroplasticity, the brain loses the ability to form new connections and process new sensory information. Connections between neurons become so weak that it becomes nearly impossible for the brain to effectively encode long-term memories; therefore, the LTP of the hippocampus declines dramatically.
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dt>
       Immune system
      </dt>
     </dl>
     <p>
      The most important aspect of the
      <a href="/wiki/Immune_system" title="Immune system">
       immune system
      </a>
      are
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/T-cells" title="T-cells">
       T-cells
      </a>
      found in the form of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/T-helper" title="T-helper">
       T-helper
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/T-suppressor_cells" title="T-suppressor cells">
       T-suppressor cells
      </a>
      . Cortisol, once released into the bloodstream, immediately begins to cause division of T-Suppressor cells. This rapid cell division increases the number of T-Suppressor cells while at the same time suppressing T-helper cells. This reduces immune protection and leaves the body vulnerable to disease and infection.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Hypothalamic-pituitary-adrenal_.28HPA.29_axis">
       Hypothalamic-pituitary-adrenal (HPA) axis
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Hypothalamic-pituitary-adrenal (HPA) axis">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Hypothalamic%E2%80%93pituitary%E2%80%93adrenal_axis" title="Hypothalamic–pituitary–adrenal axis">
       Hypothalamic–pituitary–adrenal axis
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HPA_Axis_Diagram_(Brian_M_Sweis_2012).png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="671" data-file-width="789" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/HPA_Axis_Diagram_%28Brian_M_Sweis_2012%29.png/200px-HPA_Axis_Diagram_%28Brian_M_Sweis_2012%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/HPA_Axis_Diagram_%28Brian_M_Sweis_2012%29.png/300px-HPA_Axis_Diagram_%28Brian_M_Sweis_2012%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/HPA_Axis_Diagram_%28Brian_M_Sweis_2012%29.png/400px-HPA_Axis_Diagram_%28Brian_M_Sweis_2012%29.png 2x" width="200"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HPA_Axis_Diagram_(Brian_M_Sweis_2012).png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Basic hypothalamic–pituitary–adrenal axis summary (corticotropin-releasing hormone=CRH, adrenocorticotropic hormone=ACTH).
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HPA_axis" title="HPA axis">
       HPA axis
      </a>
      is a multi-step
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biochemical_pathway" title="Biochemical pathway">
       biochemical pathway
      </a>
      where information is transmitted from one area of the body to the next via
      <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Chemical_messenger_(disambiguation)" title="Chemical messenger (disambiguation)">
       chemical messengers
      </a>
      . Each step in this pathway, as in many biochemical pathways, not only passes information along to stimulate the next region but also receives feedback from messengers produced later in the pathway to either enhance or suppress earlier steps in the pathway – this is one way a biochemical pathway can regulate itself, via a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feedback_mechanism" title="Feedback mechanism">
       feedback mechanism
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      When the hypothalamus receives signals from one of its many inputs (e.g.,
      <a href="/wiki/Cerebral_cortex" title="Cerebral cortex">
       cerebral cortex
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Limbic_system" title="Limbic system">
       limbic system
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visceral_organs" title="Visceral organs">
       visceral organs
      </a>
      ) about conditions that deviate from an ideal homeostatic state (e.g., alarming sensory stimulus, emotionally charged event, energy deficiency), this can be interpreted as the initiation step of the stress-response
      <a href="/wiki/Biochemical_cascade" title="Biochemical cascade">
       cascade
      </a>
      . The hypothalamus is stimulated by its inputs and then proceeds to secrete corticotropin-releasing hormones. This hormone is transported to its target, the pituitary gland, via the
      <a href="/wiki/Hypophyseal_portal_system" title="Hypophyseal portal system">
       hypophyseal portal system
      </a>
      (short blood vessels system), to which it binds and causes the pituitary gland to, in turn, secrete its own messenger, adrenocorticotropic hormone, systemically into the body's blood stream. When adrenocorticotropic hormone reaches and binds to its target, the adrenal gland, the adrenal gland in turn releases the final key messenger in the cascade, cortisol. Cortisol, once released, has widespread effects in the body. During an alarming situation in which a threat is detected and signaled to the hypothalamus from primary sensory and limbic structures, cortisol is one way the brain instructs the body to attempt to regain homeostasis – by redistributing energy (glucose) to areas of the body that need it most, that is, toward critical organs (the heart, the brain) and away from
      <a href="/wiki/Digestion" title="Digestion">
       digestive
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reproductive_organs" title="Reproductive organs">
       reproductive organs
      </a>
      , during a potentially harmful situation in an attempt to overcome the challenge at hand.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%D0%A5%D0%9F%D0%90-%D0%BE%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D1%81%D1%82%D1%80%D0%B5%D1%81_%D0%B2%D0%BE_%D0%BC%D0%BE%D0%B7%D0%BE%D0%BA%D0%BE%D1%82.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="540" data-file-width="509" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/%D0%A5%D0%9F%D0%90-%D0%BE%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D1%81%D1%82%D1%80%D0%B5%D1%81_%D0%B2%D0%BE_%D0%BC%D0%BE%D0%B7%D0%BE%D0%BA%D0%BE%D1%82.jpg/200px-%D0%A5%D0%9F%D0%90-%D0%BE%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D1%81%D1%82%D1%80%D0%B5%D1%81_%D0%B2%D0%BE_%D0%BC%D0%BE%D0%B7%D0%BE%D0%BA%D0%BE%D1%82.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/%D0%A5%D0%9F%D0%90-%D0%BE%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D1%81%D1%82%D1%80%D0%B5%D1%81_%D0%B2%D0%BE_%D0%BC%D0%BE%D0%B7%D0%BE%D0%BA%D0%BE%D1%82.jpg/300px-%D0%A5%D0%9F%D0%90-%D0%BE%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D1%81%D1%82%D1%80%D0%B5%D1%81_%D0%B2%D0%BE_%D0%BC%D0%BE%D0%B7%D0%BE%D0%BA%D0%BE%D1%82.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/%D0%A5%D0%9F%D0%90-%D0%BE%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D1%81%D1%82%D1%80%D0%B5%D1%81_%D0%B2%D0%BE_%D0%BC%D0%BE%D0%B7%D0%BE%D0%BA%D0%BE%D1%82.jpg/400px-%D0%A5%D0%9F%D0%90-%D0%BE%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D1%81%D1%82%D1%80%D0%B5%D1%81_%D0%B2%D0%BE_%D0%BC%D0%BE%D0%B7%D0%BE%D0%BA%D0%BE%D1%82.jpg 2x" width="200"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%D0%A5%D0%9F%D0%90-%D0%BE%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B0_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D1%81%D1%82%D1%80%D0%B5%D1%81_%D0%B2%D0%BE_%D0%BC%D0%BE%D0%B7%D0%BE%D0%BA%D0%BE%D1%82.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Schematic overview of the hypothalamic-pituary-adrenal (HPA) axis.Stress activates the HPA-axis and thereby enhances the secretion of glucocorticoids from the adrenals.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      After enough cortisol has been secreted to best restore homeostasis and the body's stressor is no longer present or the threat is no longer perceived, the heightened levels of cortisol in the body's blood stream eventually circulate to the pituitary gland and hypothalamus to which cortisol can bind and inhibit, essentially turning off the HPA-axis' stress-response cascade via
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feedback_inhibition" title="Feedback inhibition">
       feedback inhibition
      </a>
      . This prevents additional cortisol from being released. This is biologically identified as a normal, healthy stress mechanism in response to a situation or stressor – a biological coping mechanism for a threat to homeostasis.
     </p>
     <p>
      It is when the body's HPA-axis cannot overcome a challenge and/or is chronically exposed to a threat that this system becomes overtaxed and can be harmful to the body and brain. A second major effect of cortisol is to suppress the body's immune system during a stressful situation, again, for the purpose of redistributing metabolic resources primarily to fight-or-flight organs. While not a major risk to the body if only for a short period of time, if under chronic stress, the body becomes exceptionally vulnerable to immune system attacks. This is a biologically negative consequence of an exposure to a severe stressor and can be interpreted as stress in and of itself – a detrimental inability of biological mechanisms to effectively adapt to the changes in homeostasis.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Immune_response">
       Immune response
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Immune response">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation">
      <tr>
       <td class="mbox-image">
        <div style="width:52px">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg">
          <img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/>
         </a>
        </div>
       </td>
       <td class="mbox-text">
        <span class="mbox-text-span">
         This section
         <b>
          does not
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">
           cite
          </a>
          any
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">
           sources
          </a>
         </b>
         .
         <span class="hide-when-compact">
          Please help improve this section by
          <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">
           adding citations to reliable sources
          </a>
          . Unsourced material may be challenged and
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">
           removed
          </a>
          .
         </span>
         <small>
          <i>
           (April 2015)
          </i>
         </small>
         <small class="hide-when-compact">
          <i>
           (
           <a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">
            Learn how and when to remove this template message
           </a>
           )
          </i>
         </small>
        </span>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      Cortisol can weaken the activity of the
      <a href="/wiki/Immune_system" title="Immune system">
       immune system
      </a>
      . Cortisol prevents the proliferation of T-cells by rendering the interleukin-2 producer T-cells unresponsive to interleukin-1 (IL-1), and unable to produce the T-cell growth factor.[35]Cortisol also has a negative-feedback effect on interleukin-1.[36] IL-1 must be especially useful in combating some diseases; however, endotoxic bacteria have gained an advantage by forcing the hypothalamus to increase cortisol levels (forcing the secretion of CRH hormone, thus antagonizing IL-1). The suppressor cells are not affected by glucosteroid response-modifying factor (GRMF),[37] so the effective setpoint for the immune cells may be even higher than the setpoint for physiological processes (reflecting leukocyte redistribution to lymph nodes, bone marrow, and skin). Rapid administration of corticosterone (the endogenous Type I and Type II receptor agonist) orRU28362 (a specific Type II receptor agonist) to adrenalectomized animals induced changes in leukocyte distribution. Natural killer cells are not affected by cortisol.[38]
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Effect_of_stress_on_the_immune_system">
       Effect of stress on the immune system
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Effect of stress on the immune system">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Stress is the body's reaction to any stimuli that disturbs its equilibrium. When the equilibrium of various hormones is altered the effect of these changes can be detrimental to the immune system.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Khansari.2C_D._1990_45-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Khansari.2C_D._1990-45">
        [45]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Much research has shown a negative effect stress has on the immune system, mostly through studies where participants were subjected to a variety of viruses. In one study individuals caring for a spouse with dementia, representing the stress group, saw a significant decrease in immune response when given an influenza-virus vaccine compared to a non-stressed control group.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Khansari.2C_D._1990_45-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Khansari.2C_D._1990-45">
        [45]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
       <a href="#cite_note-46">
        [46]
       </a>
      </sup>
      A similar study was conducted using a respiratory virus. Participants were infected with the virus and given a stress index. Results showed that an increase in score on the stress index correlated with greater severity of cold symptoms.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Khansari.2C_D._1990_45-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Khansari.2C_D._1990-45">
        [45]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Studies with HIV have also shown stress to speed up viral progression. Men with HIV were 2–3 times more likely to develop AIDS when under above average stress.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Khansari.2C_D._1990_45-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Khansari.2C_D._1990-45">
        [45]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Stress affects the immune system in many ways. The immune system protects the body from viruses, bacteria, and anything that is different or that the body does not recognize. The immune system sees these as intruders and it sends messages to attack. The white blood cells, leukocytes, are very important to the immune system.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kalat.2C_J._W._2013_p._381_47-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Kalat.2C_J._W._2013_p._381-47">
        [47]
       </a>
      </sup>
      White blood cells have several types including B cells, T cells, and natural killer cells. B cells secrete antibodies. T cells attack intruders and natural killer cells attack cells that have been infected by viruses. These leukocytes produce cytokines which fight infections.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kalat.2C_J._W._2013_p._381_47-1">
