       <a href="#cite_note-Kalat.2C_J._W._2013_p._381-47">
        [47]
       </a>
      </sup>
      But they also are the immune systems communicator in telling the brain that the body is ill. When an individual is stressed or going through a stressful experience the immune system starts to produce natural killer cells and cytokines.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kalat.2C_J._W._2013_p._382_48-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Kalat.2C_J._W._2013_p._382-48">
        [48]
       </a>
      </sup>
      When levels of cytokines are higher they combat infections and therefore the brain gets communicated the body is ill and it produces symptoms as if the individual was ill. These symptoms include fever, sleepiness, lack of energy, no appetite, and basically flu like symptoms. These symptoms mean the body is fighting the illness or virus.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kalat.2C_J._W._2013_p._381_47-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Kalat.2C_J._W._2013_p._381-47">
        [47]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This is useful for when the body goes through the stress from an injury. But the body has now evolved to do this process during stressful events such as taking exams, or even going through a life changing event such as a death of a family member or a divorce.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kalat.2C_J._W._2013_p._382_48-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Kalat.2C_J._W._2013_p._382-48">
        [48]
       </a>
      </sup>
      That is why many times when individuals are stressed because of life changing events or situations such as those, they get these symptoms and believe they are sick when in reality it can be because the body is under stress.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kalat.2C_J._W._2013_p._382_48-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Kalat.2C_J._W._2013_p._382-48">
        [48]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_of_chronic_stress">
       Effects of chronic stress
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Effects of chronic stress">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Chronic_stress" title="Chronic stress">
       Chronic stress
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Chronic stress is defined as a "state of prolonged tension from internal or external stressors, which may cause various physical manifestations – e.g., asthma, back pain, arrhythmias, fatigue, headaches,
      <a href="/wiki/Hypertension" title="Hypertension">
       HTN
      </a>
      , irritable bowel syndrome, ulcers, and suppress the immune system".
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      Chronic stress takes a more significant toll on the body than acute stress does. It can raise blood pressure, increase the risk of heart attack and stroke, increase vulnerability to anxiety and depression, contribute to infertility, and hasten the aging process. For example, results of one study demonstrated that individuals who reported relationship conflict lasting one month or longer have a greater risk of developing illness and show slower wound healing. Similarly, the effects that acute stressors have on the immune system may be increased when there is perceived stress and/or anxiety due to other events. For example, students who are taking exams show weaker immune responses if they also report stress due to daily hassles.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graham.2C_J._2006_49-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Graham.2C_J._2006-49">
        [49]
       </a>
      </sup>
      While responses to acute stressors typically do not impose a health burden on young, healthy individuals, chronic stress in older or unhealthy individuals may have long-term effects that are detrimental to health.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
       <a href="#cite_note-50">
        [50]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanisms_of_chronic_stress">
       Mechanisms of chronic stress
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Mechanisms of chronic stress">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Studies revealing the relationship between the immune system and the central nervous system indicate that stress can alter the function of the white blood cells involved in immune function known as lymphocytes and macrophages. People undergoing stressful life events, such as marital turmoil or bereavement, have a weaker lymphoproliferative response. People in distressed marriages have also been shown to have greater decreases in cellular immunity functioning over time when compared to those in happier marriages.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
       <a href="#cite_note-51">
        [51]
       </a>
      </sup>
      After antigens initiate an immune response, these white blood cells send signals, composed of cytokines and other hormonal proteins, to the brain and neuroendocrine system.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
       <a href="#cite_note-52">
        [52]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Cytokines are molecules involved with cell signaling.
     </p>
     <p>
      Cortisol, a hormone released during stressful situations, affects the immune system greatly by preventing the production of cytokines. During chronic stress, cortisol is over produced, causing fewer receptors to be produced on immune cells so that inflammation cannot be ended. A study involving cancer patient's parents confirmed this finding. Blood samples were taken from the participants. Researchers treated the samples of the parents of cancer patients with a cortisol-like substance and stimulated cytokine production. Cancer patient parents' blood was significantly less effective at stopping cytokine from being produced.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
       <a href="#cite_note-53">
        [53]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Chronic_stress_and_wound_healing">
       Chronic stress and wound healing
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Chronic stress and wound healing">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      The immune system also plays a role in stress and the early stages of
      <a href="/wiki/Wound_healing" title="Wound healing">
       wound healing
      </a>
      . It is responsible for preparing the tissue for repair and promoting recruitment of certain cells to the wound area.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graham.2C_J._2006_49-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Graham.2C_J._2006-49">
        [49]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Consistent with the fact that stress alters the production of cytokines, Graham et al. found that chronic stress associated with care giving for a person with Alzheimer's disease leads to delayed wound healing. Results indicated that biopsy wounds healed 25% more slowly in the chronically stressed group, or those caring for a person with Alzheimer's disease.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Khansari.2C_D._1990_45-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Khansari.2C_D._1990-45">
        [45]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Chronic_stress_and_development">
       Chronic stress and development
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Chronic stress and development">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Chronic stress has also been shown to impair
      <a href="/wiki/Developmental_psychology" title="Developmental psychology">
       developmental growth
      </a>
      in children by lowering the
      <a href="/wiki/Pituitary_gland" title="Pituitary gland">
       pituitary gland
      </a>
      's production of growth hormone, as in children associated with a home environment involving serious marital discord,
      <a href="/wiki/Alcoholism" title="Alcoholism">
       alcoholism
      </a>
      , or
      <a href="/wiki/Child_abuse" title="Child abuse">
       child abuse
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
       <a href="#cite_note-54">
        [54]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Chronic_stress_and_memory">
       Chronic stress and memory
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Chronic stress and memory">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Chronic stress is seen to affect the parts of the brain where
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Memories" title="Memories">
       memories
      </a>
      are processed through and stored. When people feel stressed, stress hormones get over-secreted, which affects the brain. This secretion is made up of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glucocorticoids" title="Glucocorticoids">
       glucocorticoids
      </a>
      , including
      <a href="/wiki/Cortisol" title="Cortisol">
       cortisol
      </a>
      , which are
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steroid_hormones" title="Steroid hormones">
       steroid hormones
      </a>
      that the
      <a href="/wiki/Adrenal_gland" title="Adrenal gland">
       adrenal gland
      </a>
      releases, although this can increase storage of flashbulb memories it decreases long-term potentation (LTP).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
       <a href="#cite_note-55">
        [55]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Prolonged Stress can also be harmful to our body. That is because stress releases cortisol, and cortisol causes metabolic activity throughout the body. Metabolic activity is raised in the hippocampus. Overstimulation and toxins then are more likely to kill or damage neurons in the hippocampus.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kalat.2C_J._W._2013_p._383_56-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Kalat.2C_J._W._2013_p._383-56">
        [56]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The hippocampus is important in the brain for storing certain kinds of memories and damage to the hippocampus can cause trouble in storing new memories but old memories, memories stored before the damage, are not lost.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
       <a href="#cite_note-57">
        [57]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Also high cortisol levels can be tied to the deterioration of the hippocampus and decline of memory that many older adults start to experience with age.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kalat.2C_J._W._2013_p._383_56-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Kalat.2C_J._W._2013_p._383-56">
        [56]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Stress_and_visceral_fat">
       Stress and visceral fat
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Stress and visceral fat">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Studies of female monkeys at
      <a href="/wiki/Wake_Forest_University" title="Wake Forest University">
       Wake Forest University
      </a>
      (2009) discovered that individuals suffering from higher stress have higher levels of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visceral_fat" title="Visceral fat">
       visceral fat
      </a>
      in their bodies. This suggests a possible cause-and-effect link between the two, wherein stress promotes the accumulation of visceral fat, which in turn causes hormonal and
      <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_syndrome" title="Metabolic syndrome">
       metabolic
      </a>
      changes that contribute to heart disease and other health problems.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Time2009_58-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Time2009-58">
        [58]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Psychological_concepts">
       Psychological concepts
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Psychological concepts">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stress_(psychological)" title="Stress (psychological)">
       Stress (psychological)
      </a>
     </div>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Eustress">
       Eustress
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Eustress">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Selye" title="Selye">
       Selye
      </a>
      published in year 1975 a model dividing stress into
      <a href="/wiki/Eustress" title="Eustress">
       eustress
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Distress_(medicine)" title="Distress (medicine)">
       distress
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
       <a href="#cite_note-59">
        [59]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Where stress enhances function (physical or mental, such as through
      <a href="/wiki/Strength_training" title="Strength training">
       strength training
      </a>
      or challenging work), it may be considered eustress. Persistent stress that is not resolved through coping or adaptation, deemed distress, may lead to
      <a href="/wiki/Anxiety" title="Anxiety">
       anxiety
      </a>
      or withdrawal (depression) behavior.
     </p>
     <p>
      The difference between experiences that result in
      <i>
       eustress
      </i>
      and those that result in
      <i>
       distress
      </i>
      is determined by the disparity between an experience (real or imagined) and personal expectations, and resources to cope with the stress. Alarming experiences, either real or imagined, can trigger a stress response.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
       <a href="#cite_note-60">
        [60]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Coping">
       Coping
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Coping">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Stress_management" title="Stress management">
       Stress management
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Responses to stress include adaptation,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Psychological_coping" title="Psychological coping">
       psychological coping
      </a>
      such as
      <a href="/wiki/Stress_management" title="Stress management">
       stress management
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Anxiety" title="Anxiety">
       anxiety
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Depression_(mood)" title="Depression (mood)">
       depression
      </a>
      . Over the long term, distress can lead to diminished health and/or increased propensity to illness; to avoid this, stress must be managed.
     </p>
     <p>
      Stress management encompasses techniques intended to equip a person with effective coping mechanisms for dealing with psychological stress, with stress defined as a person's physiological response to an internal or external stimulus that triggers the fight-or-flight response. Stress management is effective when a person uses strategies to cope with or alter stressful situations.
     </p>
     <p>
      There are several ways of coping with stress,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
       <a href="#cite_note-61">
        [61]
       </a>
      </sup>
      such as controlling the source of stress or learning to set limits and to say "no" to some of the demands that bosses or family members may make.
     </p>
     <p>
      A person's capacity to tolerate the source of stress may be increased by thinking about another topic such as a hobby, listening to music, or
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Attention_Restoration_Theory" title="Attention Restoration Theory">
       spending time in a wilderness
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      A way to control stress is first dealing with what is causing the stress if it is something the individual has control over. Other methods to control stress and reduce it can be: to not procrastinate and leave tasks for last minute, do things you like, exercise, do breathing routines, go out with friends, and take a break. Having support from a loved one also helps a lot in reducing stress.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kalat.2C_J._W._2013_p._383_56-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Kalat.2C_J._W._2013_p._383-56">
        [56]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      A study was done and it showed that the power of having support from a loved one or just social support, lowered stress in the individuals. They gave painful shocks to married women's ankles. On some trials women were able to hold their husbands hand, on other trials they held a strangers hand, and then held no one's hand. When the women were holding their husbands hand, the response reduced in many brain areas. When holding the strangers hand the response reduced a little but not as much as when they were holding their husbands hand. Social support helps reduce stress but even more if the support is from a loved one.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kalat.2C_J._W._2013_p._383_56-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Kalat.2C_J._W._2013_p._383-56">
        [56]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Cognitive_appraisal">
       Cognitive appraisal
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Cognitive appraisal">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Lazarus
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
       <a href="#cite_note-62">
        [62]
       </a>
      </sup>
      argued that, in order for a psychosocial situation to be stressful, it must be appraised as such. He argued that cognitive processes of appraisal are central in determining whether a situation is potentially threatening, constitutes a harm/loss or a challenge, or is benign.
     </p>
     <p>
      Both personal and environmental factors influence this primary appraisal, which then triggers the selection of coping processes. Problem-focused coping is directed at managing the problem, whereas emotion-focused coping processes are directed at managing the negative emotions. Secondary appraisal refers to the evaluation of the resources available to cope with the problem, and may alter the primary appraisal.
     </p>
     <p>
      In other words, primary appraisal includes the perception of how stressful the problem is and the secondary appraisal of estimating whether one has more than or less than adequate resources to deal with the problem that affects the overall appraisal of stressfulness. Further, coping is flexible in that, in general, the individual examines the effectiveness of the coping on the situation; if it is not having the desired effect, s/he will, in general, try different strategies.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aldwin_63-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Aldwin-63">
        [63]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Clinical_symptoms_and_disorders">
       Clinical symptoms and disorders
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Clinical symptoms and disorders">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Symptoms">
       Symptoms
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Symptoms">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Signs of stress may be cognitive, emotional, physical, or behavioral.
     </p>
     <p>
      <b>
       Cognitive symptoms
      </b>
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       Memory problems
      </li>
      <li>
       Inability to concentrate
      </li>
      <li>
       Poor judgment
      </li>
      <li>
       Pessimistic approach or thoughts
      </li>
      <li>
       Anxious or racing thoughts
      </li>
      <li>
       Constant worrying
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      <b>
       Emotional symptoms
      </b>
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       Moodiness
      </li>
      <li>
       Irritability or short temper
      </li>
      <li>
       Agitation, inability to relax
      </li>
      <li>
       Feeling overwhelmed
      </li>
      <li>
       Sense of loneliness and isolation
      </li>
      <li>
       Depression or general unhappiness
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      <b>
       Physical symptoms
      </b>
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       Aches and pains
      </li>
      <li>
       Diarrhea or constipation
      </li>
      <li>
       Increased frequency of urination
      </li>
      <li>
       Indigestion
      </li>
      <li>
       Changes in blood glucose
      </li>
      <li>
       Nausea, dizziness
      </li>
      <li>
       Chest pain, rapid heartbeat
      </li>
      <li>
       Loss of sex drive
      </li>
      <li>
       Frequent colds
      </li>
      <li>
       Irregular periods.
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      <b>
       Behavioral symptoms
      </b>
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       Eating more or less
      </li>
      <li>
       Sleeping too much or too little
      </li>
      <li>
       Isolating oneself from others
      </li>
      <li>
       Procrastinating or neglecting responsibilities
      </li>
      <li>
       Using drugs such as alcohol or cigarettes to relax
      </li>
      <li>
       Nervous habits (e.g. nail biting, pacing)
      </li>
     </ul>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="DSM-IV_TR">
       DSM-IV TR
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: DSM-IV TR">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Diagnosis">
       Diagnosis
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Diagnosis">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main articles:
      <a href="/wiki/Holmes_and_Rahe_stress_scale" title="Holmes and Rahe stress scale">
       Holmes and Rahe stress scale
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stress_(psychological)" title="Stress (psychological)">
       Stress (psychological)
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      A renewed interest in salivary alpha
      <a href="/wiki/Amylase" title="Amylase">
       amylase
      </a>
      as a marker for stress has surfaced. Yamaguchi M, Yoshida H (2005) have analyzed a newly introduced hand-held device called the Cocorometer developed by
      <a href="/wiki/Nipro" title="Nipro">
       Nipro Corporation
      </a>
      of Japan. They state that this can be reliably used to analyze the amylase levels and is definitely a cheaper alternative as compared to the more expensive ELISA kits. The working consists of a meter and a saliva collecting chip, which can be inserted into the meter to give the readings. The levels of amylase obtained have been calibrated according to standard population, and can be categorized into four levels of severity.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
       <a href="#cite_note-64">
        [64]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Measuring stress levels independent of differences in people's personalities has been inherently difficult: Some people are able to process many stressors simultaneously, while others can barely address a few. Such tests as the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trier_Social_Stress_Test" title="Trier Social Stress Test">
       Trier Social Stress Test
      </a>
      attempted to isolate the effects of personalities on ability to handle stress in a laboratory environment. Other psychologists, however, proposed measuring stress indirectly, through self-tests.
     </p>
     <p>
      Because the amount of stressors in a person's life often (although not always) correlates with the amount of stress that person experiences, researchers combine the results of stress and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burnout_(psychology)" title="Burnout (psychology)">
       burnout
      </a>
      self-tests. Stress tests help determine the number of stressors in a person's life, while burnout tests determine the degree to which the person is close to the state of burnout. Combining both helps researchers gauge how likely additional stressors will make him or her experience mental exhaustion.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Truby_65-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Truby-65">
        [65]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Health_risk_factors">
       Health risk factors
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Health risk factors">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Both negative and positive stressors can lead to stress. The intensity and duration of stress changes depending on the circumstances and emotional condition of the person suffering from it (Arnold. E and Boggs. K. 2007). Some common categories and examples of stressors include:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       Sensory input such as
       <a href="/wiki/Pain" title="Pain">
        pain
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Over-illumination" title="Over-illumination">
        bright light
       </a>
       , noise, temperatures, or environmental issues such as a lack of control over environmental circumstances, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Food" title="Food">
        food
       </a>
       , air and/or water quality,
       <a href="/wiki/House" title="House">
        housing
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Health" title="Health">
        health
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Liberty" title="Liberty">
        freedom
       </a>
       , or mobility.
      </li>
      <li>
       Social issues can also cause stress, such as struggles with
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conspecific" title="Conspecific">
        conspecific
       </a>
       or difficult individuals and
       <a href="/wiki/Social_defeat" title="Social defeat">
        social defeat
       </a>
       , or relationship conflict,
       <a href="/wiki/Deception" title="Deception">
        deception
       </a>
       , or
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Break_up" title="Break up">
        break ups
       </a>
       , and major events such as
       <a href="/wiki/Birth" title="Birth">
        birth
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Death" title="Death">
        deaths
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Marriage" title="Marriage">
        marriage
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Divorce" title="Divorce">
        divorce
       </a>
       .
      </li>
      <li>
       Life experiences such as
       <a href="/wiki/Poverty" title="Poverty">
        poverty
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Unemployment" title="Unemployment">
        unemployment
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clinical_depression" title="Clinical depression">
        clinical depression
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Obsessive_compulsive_disorder" title="Obsessive compulsive disorder">
        obsessive compulsive disorder
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Health#Heavy_consumption" title="Health">
        heavy drinking
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glavas_66-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Glavas-66">
         [66]
        </a>
       </sup>
       or insufficient
       <a href="/wiki/Sleep" title="Sleep">
        sleep
       </a>
       can also cause stress. Students and workers may face performance pressure stress from
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Test_(student_assessment)" title="Test (student assessment)">
        exams
       </a>
       and project deadlines.
      </li>
      <li>
       Adverse experiences during
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Developmental_Psychology" title="Developmental Psychology">
        development
       </a>
       (e.g. prenatal exposure to maternal stress,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
        <a href="#cite_note-67">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
        <a href="#cite_note-68">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
       poor attachment histories,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schore_69-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Schore-69">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Sexual_abuse" title="Sexual abuse">
        sexual abuse
       </a>
       )
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
        <a href="#cite_note-70">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
       are thought to contribute to deficits in the maturity of an individual's stress response systems. One evaluation of the different stresses in people's lives is the
       <a href="/wiki/Holmes_and_Rahe_stress_scale" title="Holmes and Rahe stress scale">
        Holmes and Rahe stress scale
       </a>
       .
      </li>
     </ul>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Generalized_anxiety_disorder">
       Generalized anxiety disorder
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Generalized anxiety disorder">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Generalized_anxiety_disorder" title="Generalized anxiety disorder">
       Generalized anxiety disorder
      </a>
     </div>
     <dl>
      <dt>
       <b>
        Areas of the brain affected
       </b>
      </dt>
     </dl>
     <p>
      During passive activity, patients with generalised anxiety disorder (GAD) exhibit increased metabolic rates in the occipital, temporal and frontal lobes and in the cerebellum and thalamus compared with healthy controls. Increased metabolic activity in the basal ganglia has also been reported in patients with GAD during vigilance tasks. These finding suggest that there may be hyperactive brain circuits in GAD.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
       <a href="#cite_note-71">
        [71]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Patients with generalised anxiety disorder (GAD) exhibit increased metabolic rates in several brain regions compared with healthy controls. Hyperactive neurotransmitter circuits between the cortex, thalamus, amygdala and hypothalamus have been implicated in the disorder. Hypofunction of serotonergic neurones arising from the dorsal raphe nucleus and GABAergic neurones that are widely distributed in the brain may result in a lack of inhibitory effect on the putative GAD pathway. Furthermore, overactivity of noradrenergic neurones arising from the locus coeruleus may produce excessive excitation in the brain areas implicated in GAD.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
       <a href="#cite_note-72">
        [72]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dt>
       <b>
        Septohippocampal circuit
       </b>
      </dt>
     </dl>
     <p>
      Based on early neuroanatomical observations and studies with psychoactive drugs, the septohippocampal circuit has been proposed as a model for anxiety disorders. The circuit that links the septum, amygdala, hippocampus and fornix is thought to process external stimuli and regulate the behavioural response through wider projections in the brain. Hyperstimulation of this putative ‘behavioural inhibition' circuit, through dysfunctional noradrenergic and serotonergic neurotransmission, has been implicated in producing anxiety, and increased arousal and attention.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
       <a href="#cite_note-73">
        [73]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dt>
       <b>
        Noradrenaline pathways
       </b>
      </dt>
     </dl>
     <p>
      In generalised anxiety disorder (GAD) there is increased noradrenaline transmission from both the locus coeruleus and the caudal raphe nuclei. The locus coeruleus-noradrenaline system is associated with anxiety and may mediate the autonomic symptoms associated with stress such as increased heart rate, dilated pupils, tremour and sweating.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
       <a href="#cite_note-74">
        [74]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dt>
       <b>
        Serotonergic pathways
       </b>
      </dt>
     </dl>
     <p>
      Serotonergic nuclei are found in the rostral and caudal raphe nuclei. Neurones ascend from the rostral raphe nuclei to the cerebral cortex, limbic regions and basal ganglia. The activity of neurones innervating the pre-frontal cortex, basal ganglia and limbic region is decreased in generalised anxiety disorder (GAD). The activity of descending neurones from serotonergic nuclei in the brainstem is unaffected in GAD. This altered neurotransmitter balance contributes towards the feeling of anxiety associated with GAD.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
       <a href="#cite_note-75">
        [75]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dt>
       <b>
        GABAergic pathways
       </b>
      </dt>
     </dl>
     <p>
      GABA is the main inhibitory neurotransmitter in the central nervous system (CNS). GABAergic inhibition is seen at all levels of the CNS, including the hypothalamus, hippocampus, cerebral cortex and cerebellar cortex. The activity of GABAergic neurones is decreased in generalised anxiety disorder.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
       <a href="#cite_note-76">
        [76]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Panic_disorder">
       Panic disorder
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Panic disorder">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Panic_disorder" title="Panic disorder">
       Panic disorder
      </a>
     </div>
     <dl>
      <dt>
       <b>
        The areas of the brain affected in panic disorder
       </b>
      </dt>
     </dl>
     <p>
      There are a number of areas of the brain affected in panic disorder. Decreased serotonin activity in the amygdala and frontal cortex induces symptoms of anxiety, whereas decreased activity in the
      <a href="/wiki/Periaqueductal_gray" title="Periaqueductal gray">
       periaqueductal gray
      </a>
      results in defensive behaviours and postural freezing. The locus coeruleus increases norepinephrine release mediating physiological and behavioural arousal, while the hypothalamus mediates the sympathetic nervous system.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_77-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-77">
        [77]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_78-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-78">
        [78]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Am_J_Psychiatry_2000.3B157:493.E2.80.93505_79-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Am_J_Psychiatry_2000.3B157:493.E2.80.93505-79">
        [79]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dt>
       <b>
        The areas of the brain affected in panic disorder (advanced)
       </b>
      </dt>
     </dl>
     <p>
      Hyperactive neurotransmitter circuits between the cortex, thalamus, hippocampus, amygdala, hypothalamus and periaqueductal gray matter have been implicated in panic disorder. Hypofunction of serotonergic neurones arising from the rostral raphe nucleus may result in a lack of inhibitory effect on the putative panic pathways in the brain. While, overactivity of norepinephrine neurons arising from the locus coeruleus may produce excessive excitation in the regions implicated in panic disorder. Physiological symptoms of the panic response are medicated by the autonomic nervous system through connections with the locus coeruleus and hypothalamus.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_77-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-77">
        [77]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_78-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-78">
        [78]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Am_J_Psychiatry_2000.3B157:493.E2.80.93505_79-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Am_J_Psychiatry_2000.3B157:493.E2.80.93505-79">
        [79]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_80-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-80">
        [80]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ClinPsychiatry_81-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ClinPsychiatry-81">
        [81]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dt>
       <b>
        The serotonin pathways in panic disorder
       </b>
      </dt>
     </dl>
     <p>
      The principal serotonin centres in the brain are the caudal and rostral raphe nuclei. Transmission of serotonin from the rostral raphe nuclei to the pre-aquaductal grey, amygdala, temporal lobe and limbic cortex is decreased in panic disorder compared with normal. Serotonin transmission to other target regions of the brain remain unchanged.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jcp1997_82-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-jcp1997-82">
        [82]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dt>
       <b>
        The norepinephrine pathways in panic disorder
       </b>
      </dt>
     </dl>
     <p>
      In panic disorder there is increased norepinephrine transmission from both the locus coeruleus and the caudal raphe nuclei. The locus coeruleus-norepinephrine system may have a significant role in processing fear-related stimuli or it may affect fear-related processing by stimulating other regions of the brain implicated in anxiety and fear behaviours i.e. amygdala, hippocampus, hypothalamus, cortex and spinal cord.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jcp1997_82-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-jcp1997-82">
        [82]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="General_adaptation_syndrome">
       General adaptation syndrome
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: General adaptation syndrome">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:General_Adaptation_Syndrome.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="630" data-file-width="864" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/General_Adaptation_Syndrome.jpg/300px-General_Adaptation_Syndrome.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/General_Adaptation_Syndrome.jpg/450px-General_Adaptation_Syndrome.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/General_Adaptation_Syndrome.jpg/600px-General_Adaptation_Syndrome.jpg 2x" width="300"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:General_Adaptation_Syndrome.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A diagram of the General Adaptation Syndrome model.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Physiologists define stress as how the body reacts to a stressor, real or imagined, a stimulus that causes stress. Acute stressors affect an organism in the short term; chronic stressors over the longer term. General Adaptation Syndrome (GAS), developed by Hans Selye, is a profile of how organisms respond to stress; GAS is characterized by three phases: a nonspecific mobilization phase, which promotes sympathetic nervous system activity; a resistance phase, during which the organism makes efforts to cope with the threat; and an exhaustion phase, which occurs if the organism fails to overcome the threat and depletes its physiological resources.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
       <a href="#cite_note-83">
        [83]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Stage_1">
       Stage 1
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Stage 1">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      <b>
       Alarm
      </b>
      is the first stage, which is divided into two phases: the
      <i>
       shock
      </i>
      phase and the
      <i>
       antishock
      </i>
      phase.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
       <a href="#cite_note-84">
        [84]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <i>
        Shock phase
       </i>
       : During this phase, the body can endure changes such as
       <a href="/wiki/Hypovolemia" title="Hypovolemia">
        hypovolemia
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Osmotic_concentration" title="Osmotic concentration">
        hypoosmolarity
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Hyponatremia" title="Hyponatremia">
        hyponatremia
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Hypochloremia" title="Hypochloremia">
        hypochloremia
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Hypoglycemia" title="Hypoglycemia">
        hypoglycemia
       </a>
       —the stressor effect. This phase resembles
       <a href="/wiki/Addison%27s_disease" title="Addison's disease">
        Addison's disease
       </a>
       . The organism's resistance to the stressor drops temporarily below the normal range and some level of shock (e.g.
       <a href="/wiki/Shock_(circulatory)" title="Shock (circulatory)">
        circulatory shock
       </a>
       ) may be experienced.
      </li>
      <li>
       <i>
        Antishock phase
       </i>
       : When the threat or stressor is identified or realized, the body starts to respond and is in a state of alarm. During this stage, the
       <a href="/wiki/Locus_coeruleus" title="Locus coeruleus">
        locus coeruleus
       </a>
       /sympathetic nervous system is activated and catecholamines such as adrenaline are being produced, hence the
       <a href="/wiki/Fight-or-flight_response" title="Fight-or-flight response">
        fight-or-flight response
       </a>
       . The result is: increased
       <a href="/wiki/Muscle_tone" title="Muscle tone">
        muscular tonus
       </a>
       , increased blood pressure due to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peripheral_vasoconstriction" title="Peripheral vasoconstriction">
        peripheral vasoconstriction
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Tachycardia" title="Tachycardia">
        tachycardia
       </a>
       , and increased glucose in blood. There is also some activation of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypothalamic-pituitary-adrenal_axis" title="Hypothalamic-pituitary-adrenal axis">
        HPA axis
       </a>
       , producing glucocorticoids (
       <a href="/wiki/Cortisol" title="Cortisol">
        cortisol
       </a>
       , aka the S-hormone or stress-hormone).
      </li>
     </ul>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Stage_2">
       Stage 2
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Stage 2">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      <b>
       Resistance
      </b>
      is the second stage and increased secretion of glucocorticoids play a major role, intensifying the systemic response—they have lipolytic, catabolic and antianabolic effects: increased glucose, fat and aminoacid/protein concentration in blood. Moreover, they cause lymphocytopenia, eosinopenia, neutrophilia and polycythemia. In high doses, cortisol begins to act as a mineralocorticoid (aldosterone) and brings the body to a state similar to
      <a href="/wiki/Hyperaldosteronism" title="Hyperaldosteronism">
       hyperaldosteronism
      </a>
      . If the stressor persists, it becomes necessary to attempt some means of coping with the stress. Although the body begins to try to adapt to the strains or demands of the environment, the body cannot keep this up indefinitely, so its resources are gradually depleted.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Stage_3">
       Stage 3
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Stage 3">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      The third stage could be either
      <i>
       exhaustion
      </i>
      or
      <i>
       recovery
      </i>
      :
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <b>
        Recovery
       </b>
       stage follows when the system's compensation mechanisms have successfully overcome the stressor effect (or have completely eliminated the factor which caused the stress). The high glucose, fat and aminoacid levels in blood prove useful for anabolic reactions, restoration of homeostasis and regeneration of cells.
      </li>
      <li>
       <b>
        Exhaustion
       </b>
       is the alternative third stage in the GAS model. At this point, all of the body's resources are eventually depleted and the body is unable to maintain normal function. The initial
       <a href="/wiki/Autonomic_nervous_system" title="Autonomic nervous system">
        autonomic nervous system
       </a>
       symptoms may reappear (sweating, raised heart rate, etc.). If stage three is extended, long-term damage may result (prolonged vasoconstriction results in
       <a href="/wiki/Ischemia" title="Ischemia">
        ischemia
       </a>
       which in turn leads to cell necrosis), as the body's immune system becomes exhausted, and bodily functions become impaired, resulting in
       <a href="/wiki/Decompensation" title="Decompensation">
        decompensation
       </a>
       .
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      The result can manifest itself in obvious illnesses, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Peptic_ulcer" title="Peptic ulcer">
       peptic ulcer
      </a>
      and general trouble with the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Digestive_system" title="Digestive system">
       digestive system
      </a>
      (e.g.
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Occult_bleeding" title="Occult bleeding">
       occult bleeding
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Melena" title="Melena">
       melena
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Constipation" title="Constipation">
       constipation
      </a>
      /obstipation),
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diabetes" title="Diabetes">
       diabetes
      </a>
      , or even
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cardiovascular" title="Cardiovascular">
       cardiovascular
      </a>
      problems (
      <a href="/wiki/Angina_pectoris" title="Angina pectoris">
       angina pectoris
      </a>
      ), along with
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clinical_depression" title="Clinical depression">
       clinical depression
      </a>
      and other mental illnesses.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="Reliable sources needed for individual assertions (August 2016)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Phobia">
       Phobia
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Phobia">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <dl>
      <dt>
       <b>
        The areas of the brain affected in phobia
       </b>
      </dt>
     </dl>
     <p>
      There are a number of areas of the brain affected in phobia. Activation of the amygdala causes anticipatory anxiety or avoidance (conditioned fear) while activation of the hypothalamus activates the sympathetic nervous system. Other regions of the brain involved in phobia include the thalamus and the cortical structures, which may form a key neural network along with the amygdala. Stimulation of the locus coeruleus increases noradrenaline release mediating physiological and behavioural arousal.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Am_J_Psychiatry_2000.3B157:493.E2.80.93505_79-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Am_J_Psychiatry_2000.3B157:493.E2.80.93505-79">
        [79]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dt>
       <b>
        The noradrenaline pathways in phobia
       </b>
      </dt>
     </dl>
     <p>
      One hypothesis about the biological basis of phobia suggests that there is an excess of noradrenaline in the principal noradrenergic pathways in the brain and that this causes a down-regulation of post-synaptic adrenergic receptors. Transmission of noradrenaline from the caudal raphe nuclei and the locus coeruleus is increased in phobia.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Drugtreatments_85-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Drugtreatments-85">
        [85]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dt>
       <b>
        The serotonin pathways in phobia
       </b>
      </dt>
     </dl>
     <p>
      The principal centres in the brain are the caudal and rostral raphe nuclei. Transmission of serotonin from the rostral raphe nuclei to the thalamus, limbic cortex and cerebral cortex is decreased in phobia compared with normal. The other major pathways for serotonin transmission which involve the basal ganglia and cerebellum, and project down the spinal cord, remain unchanged.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Drugtreatments_85-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Drugtreatments-85">
        [85]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Post-traumatic_stress_disorder_.28PTSD.29">
       Post-traumatic stress disorder (PTSD)
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Post-traumatic stress disorder (PTSD)">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Posttraumatic_stress_disorder" title="Posttraumatic stress disorder">
       Posttraumatic stress disorder
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PTSD_stress_brain.gif">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="365" data-file-width="368" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/cc/PTSD_stress_brain.gif/220px-PTSD_stress_brain.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/cc/PTSD_stress_brain.gif/330px-PTSD_stress_brain.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/c/cc/PTSD_stress_brain.gif 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PTSD_stress_brain.gif" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Regions of the brain associated with stress and posttraumatic stress disorder
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
         <a href="#cite_note-86">
          [86]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      PTSD is a severe anxiety disorder that can develop after exposure to any event that results in psychological trauma. This event may involve the threat of death to oneself or to someone else, or to one's own or someone else's physical, sexual, or psychological integrity, overwhelming the individual's ability to cope. As an effect of psychological trauma, PTSD is less frequent and more enduring than the more commonly seen
      <a href="/wiki/Acute_stress_reaction" title="Acute stress reaction">
       acute stress response
      </a>
      . Diagnostic symptoms for PTSD include
      <b>
       intrusion, avoidance and hyperarousal
      </b>
      -- re-experiencing the original trauma(s) through "flashbacks" or nightmares (
      <i>
       intrusion
      </i>
      ), emotional numbing or avoidance of stimuli associated with the trauma, and increased arousal, such as difficulty falling or staying asleep, anger, and hypervigilance. Formal diagnostic criteria (both DSM-IV-TR and ICD-10) require that the symptoms last more than one month and cause significant impairment in social, occupational, or other important areas of functioning.
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dt>
       <b>
        The areas of the brain affected in post-traumatic stress disorder
       </b>
      </dt>
     </dl>
     <p>
      Sensory input, memory formation and stress response mechanisms are affected in people with PTSD. The regions of the brain involved in memory processing that are implicated in PTSD include the hippocampus, amygdala and frontal cortex. While the heightened stress response is likely to involve the thalamus, hypothalamus and locus coeruleus.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_80-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-80">
        [80]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ClinPsychiatry_81-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-ClinPsychiatry-81">
        [81]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dt>
       <b>
        Memory
       </b>
      </dt>
     </dl>
     <p>
      Cortisol works with epinephrine (adrenaline) to create memories of short-term emotional events; this is the proposed mechanism for storage of flash bulb memories, and may originate as a means to remember what to avoid in the future. However, long-term exposure to cortisol damages cells in the hippocampus; this damage results in impaired learning. Furthermore, it has been shown that cortisol inhibits memory retrieval of already stored information.
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dt>
       <b>
        Atrophy of the hippocampus in posttraumatic stress disorder
       </b>
      </dt>
     </dl>
     <p>
      There is consistent evidence from MRI volumetric studies that hippocampal volume is reduced in posttraumatic stress disorder (PTSD). This atrophy of the hippocampus is thought to represent decreased neuronal density. However, other studies suggest that hippocampal changes are explained by whole brain atrophy and generalised white matter atrophy is exhibited by people with PTSD.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
       <a href="#cite_note-87">
        [87]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
       <a href="#cite_note-88">
        [88]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Depression">
       Depression
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Depression">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <dl>
      <dt>
       <b>
        The areas of the brain affected in depression
       </b>
      </dt>
     </dl>
     <p>
      Many areas of the brain appear to be involved in
      <a href="/wiki/Depression_(mood)" title="Depression (mood)">
       depression
      </a>
      including the frontal and temporal lobes and parts of the limbic system including the cingulate gyrus. However, it is not clear if the changes in these areas cause depression or if the disturbance occurs as a result of the etiology of psychiatric disorders.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
       <a href="#cite_note-89">
        [89]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dt>
       <b>
        The hypothalamic-pituitary-adrenal (HPA) axis in depression
       </b>
      </dt>
     </dl>
     <p>
      In depression, the
      <a href="/wiki/Hypothalamic%E2%80%93pituitary%E2%80%93adrenal_axis" title="Hypothalamic–pituitary–adrenal axis">
       hypothalamic-pituitary-adrenal (HPA) axis
      </a>
      is upregulated with a down-regulation of its negative feedback controls. Corticotropin-releasing factor (CRF) is hypersecreted from the hypothalamus and induces the release of adrenocorticotropin hormone (ACTH) from the pituitary. ACTH interacts with receptors on adrenocortical cells and cortisol is released from the adrenal glands; adrenal hypertrophy can also occur. Release of cortisol into the circulation has a number of effects, including elevation of blood glucose. The negative feedback of cortisol to the hypothalamus, pituitary and immune system is impaired. This leads to continual activation of the HPA axis and excess cortisol release. Cortisol receptors become desensitized leading to increased activity of the pro-inflammatory immune mediators and disturbances in neurotransmitter transmission.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
       <a href="#cite_note-90">
        [90]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
       <a href="#cite_note-91">
        [91]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">
       <a href="#cite_note-92">
        [92]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93">
       <a href="#cite_note-93">
        [93]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dt>
       <b>
        The serotonin pathways in depression
       </b>
      </dt>
     </dl>
     <p>
      Serotonin transmission from both the caudal raphe nuclei and rostral raphe nuclei is reduced in patients with depression compared with non-depressed controls. Increasing the levels of serotonin in these pathways, by reducing serotonin reuptake and hence increasing serotonin function, is one of the therapeutic approaches to treating depression.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">
       <a href="#cite_note-94">
        [94]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dt>
       <b>
        The noradrenaline pathways in depression
       </b>
      </dt>
     </dl>
     <p>
      In depression the transmission of noradrenaline is reduced from both of the principal noradrenergic centres – the locus coeruleus and the caudal raphe nuclei. An increase in noradrenaline in the frontal/prefrontal cortex modulates the action of selective noradrenaline reuptake inhibition and improves mood. Increasing noradrenaline transmission to other areas of the frontal cortex modulates attention.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95">
       <a href="#cite_note-95">
        [95]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="History_in_research">
       History in research
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stress_(biology)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: History in research">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      The current usage of the word
      <i>
       stress
      </i>
      arose out of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Selye" title="Selye">
       Selye
      </a>
      's 1930s experiments. He started to use the term to refer not just to the agent but to the state of the organism as it responded and adapted to the environment. His theories of a universal non-specific stress response attracted great interest and contention in academic
      <a href="/wiki/Physiology" title="Physiology">
       physiology
      </a>
      and he undertook extensive research programs and publication efforts.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Viner1999_96-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Viner1999-96">
        [96]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      While the work attracted continued support from advocates of
      <a href="/wiki/Psychosomatic_medicine" title="Psychosomatic medicine">
       psychosomatic medicine
      </a>
      , many in experimental physiology concluded that his concepts were too vague and unmeasurable. During the 1950s, Selye turned away from the laboratory to promote his concept through popular books and lecture tours. He wrote for both non-academic physicians and, in an international bestseller entitled
      <i>
       Stress of Life
      </i>
      , for the general public.
     </p>
     <p>
      A broad
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biopsychosocial" title="Biopsychosocial">
       biopsychosocial
      </a>
      concept of stress and adaptation offered the promise of helping everyone achieve health and happiness by successfully responding to changing global challenges and the problems of modern
      <a href="/wiki/Civilization" title="Civilization">
       civilization
      </a>
      . Selye coined the term "
      <a href="/wiki/Eustress" title="Eustress">
       eustress
      </a>
      " for positive stress, by contrast to
      <a href="/wiki/Distress_(medicine)" title="Distress (medicine)">
       distress
      </a>
      . He argued that all people have a natural urge and need to work for their own benefit, a message that found favor with industrialists and governments.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Viner1999_96-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Viner1999-96">
        [96]
       </a>
      </sup>
      He also coined the term
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Stressor" title="Stressor">
        stressor
       </a>
      </i>
      to refer to the causative event or stimulus, as opposed to the resulting state of stress.
     </p>
     <p>
      From the late 1960s, academic
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Psychologists" title="Psychologists">
       psychologists
      </a>
      started to adopt
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Selye" title="Selye">
       Selye
      </a>
      's concept; they sought to quantify "life stress" by scoring "
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Life-Events_and_Difficulties_Schedule" title="Life-Events and Difficulties Schedule">
       significant life events
      </a>
      ," and a large amount of research was undertaken to examine links between stress and disease of all kinds. By the late 1970s, stress had become the medical area of greatest concern to the general population, and more basic research was called for to better address the issue. There was also renewed laboratory research into the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neuroendocrine" title="Neuroendocrine">
       neuroendocrine
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecular" title="Molecular">
       molecular
      </a>
      , and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immunological" title="Immunological">
       immunological
      </a>
      bases of stress, conceived as a useful
      <a href="/wiki/Heuristic" title="Heuristic">
       heuristic
      </a>
      not necessarily tied to
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Selye" title="Selye">
       Selye
      </a>
      's original hypotheses. The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_military" title="US military">
       US military
      </a>
      became a key center of stress research, attempting to understand and reduce combat
      <a href="/wiki/Neurosis" title="Neurosis">
       neurosis
      </a>
      and psychiatric casualties.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Viner1999_96-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Viner1999-96">
        [96]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Psychiatric" title="Psychiatric">
       psychiatric
      </a>
      diagnosis
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Post-traumatic_stress_disorder" title="Post-traumatic stress disorder">
        post-traumatic stress disorder
       </a>
      </i>
      (
      <i>
       PTSD
      </i>
      ) was coined in the mid-1970s, in part through the efforts of anti-Vietnam War activists and the
      <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_Veterans_Against_the_War" title="Vietnam Veterans Against the War">
       Vietnam Veterans Against the War
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Chaim_F._Shatan" title="Chaim F. Shatan">
       Chaim F. Shatan
      </a>
      . The condition was added to the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Diagnostic_and_Statistical_Manual_of_Mental_Disorders" title="Diagnostic and Statistical Manual of Mental Disorders">
        Diagnostic and Statistical Manual of Mental Disorders
       </a>
      </i>
      as
      <i>
       posttraumatic stress disorder
      </i>
      in 1980.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IHHRT_97-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-IHHRT-97">
        [97]
       </a>
      </sup>
      PTSD was considered a severe and ongoing emotional reaction to an extreme psychological trauma, and as such often associated with soldiers, police officers, and other emergency personnel. The stressor may involve threat to life (or viewing the actual death of someone else), serious physical injury, or threat to physical or psychological integrity. In some cases, it can also be from profound psychological and emotional trauma, apart from any actual physical harm or threat. Often, however, the two are combined.
     </p>
     <p>
      By the 1990s, "stress" had become an integral part of modern scientific understanding in all areas of physiology and human functioning, and one of the great metaphors of Western life. Focus grew on stress in certain settings, such as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Workplace_stress" title="Workplace stress">
       workplace stress
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Stress_management" title="Stress management">
       stress management
      </a>
      techniques were developed. The term also became a
      <a href="/wiki/Euphemism" title="Euphemism">
       euphemism
      </a>
      , a way of referring to problems and eliciting
      <a href="/wiki/Sympathy" title="Sympathy">
       sympathy
      </a>
      without being explicitly confessional, just "stressed out." It came to cover a huge range of phenomena from mild
      <a href="/wiki/Irritation" title="Irritation">
       irritation
      </a>
      to the kind of severe problems that might result in a real breakdown of
      <a href="/wiki/Health" title="Health">
       health
      </a>
      . In popular usage, almost any event or situation between these extremes could be described as stressful.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keil2004_21-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Keil2004-21">
        [21]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Viner1999_96-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Viner1999-96">
        [96]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
